The Debate on Cursing Mu'awiyah and Yazid
Discussion of cursing Mu'awiyah and differing views on Yazid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference of opinion among scholars regarding cursing Mu'awiyah and Yazid, and the principles behind those rulings.

Core Idea
The Salaf did not permit cursing Mu'awiyah; scholars differed on Yazid due to the killing of al-Husayn.

This lesson continues the excerpt from Sharh al-'Aqa'id, which addresses the specific cases of Mu'awiyah and Yazid. The passage notes that the early scholars did not permit cursing Mu'awiyah, as his transgression did not warrant cursing. Regarding Yazid, there is a difference: some prohibit cursing based on a general prohibition, while others permit it due to his supposed disbelief in ordering the killing of al-Husayn.

Guided Path

Read the passage and translation

The passage says 'لا يوجب اللعن' — transgression does not necessitate cursing.
3
Analyze the two views on Yazid
Distinguish the opinion that prohibits cursing Yazid (based on the Prophet's prohibition) from the opinion that permits it (due to his order killing al-Husayn considered kufr).
The first opinion also extends to al-Hajjaj.

The Necessity of Proof Before Accusation
Author criticizes a report and cites Ghazali on verification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author critiques a previous transmission for lacking clear attribution and why Ghazali insists on definitive proof before accusing a Muslim of a major sin.

Core Idea
Accusing a Muslim of a major sin requires certain knowledge, not speculation.

The author begins by pointing out the weakness of a previous report—its source is vague and its reasoning rests on unproven premises. To reinforce this, he quotes Ghazali's 'Ihya' which lays down a foundational principle: one cannot attribute a heinous act like killing al-Husayn to Yazid without clear evidence. Ghazali distinguishes between established historical facts and unverified claims, cautioning against reckless accusations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's critique
Notice that the author first criticizes the earlier transmission for being vague about its speaker and for requiring unproven premises. This sets up the need for verification.
2
Examine Ghazali's ruling
Read the quote from Ghazali. He says that since it is not established that Yazid killed or ordered the killing of al-Husayn, one cannot even say he did, let alone curse him. He contrasts this with verified cases like the killers of 'Ali and 'Umar.
Ghazali uses the term 'tawatur' (widespread transmission) to describe what constitutes proof.
3
Understand the principle
Ghazali concludes that it is forbidden to accuse any Muslim of fisq (wickedness) or kufr without verification. This is the core lesson: accusations require certainty.
4
Reflect on the danger
The author then notes Ghazali's summary: cursing specific individuals is dangerous and should be avoided; even refraining from cursing Iblis is safer.
This highlights that caution is preferable even in cases of known evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يخفى ما في نقله حيث أبهم في قائله، ثم تعليله يحتاج إلى إثبات أمره بقتل الحسين رضي الله عنه أولاً ثم ترتب كفره عليه ثانياً، وكلاهما ممنوع. فقد قال حجة الإسلام في "الإحياء": «فإن قيل: هل يجوز لعن يزيد؛ لأنه قاتل الحسين أو آمر به؟ قلنا هذا لم يثبت أصلا، فلا يجوز أن يقال: إنه قتله أو أمر به فضلا عن اللعنة، لأنه لا تجوز نسبة مسلم إلى كبيرة من غير تحقيق. نعم يجوز أن يقال : قتل ابن ملجم عليا رضي الله عنه وقتل أبو لؤلؤة عمر رضي الله عنه، فإن ذلك ثبت متواترا... فلا يرمى مسلم بفسق أو كفر من غير تحقيق». وعلى الجملة ففي لعن الأشخاص خطر فليجتنب، ولا خطر في السكوت عن لعن إبليس فضلا عن غيره، انتهى. ولأن الأمر بقتل الحسين رضي الله عنه لا يوجب الكفر، فإن قتل غير الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام كبيرة عند أهل السنة والجماعة إلا أن يكون مستحلا، وهو غير مختص بالحسين ونحوه مع أن الاستحلال أمر لا يطلع عليه إلا ذو الجلال، وإنما كان قتله نظير قتل عمار بن ياسر. وأما ما تفوه به بعض الجهلة من أن الحسين كان باغيا فباطل عند أهل السنة والجماعة، ولعل هذا من هذيانات الخوارج عن الجادة. ثم قال: واتفقوا على جواز اللعن على من قتله أو أمر به أو أجازه أو رضي به، ففيه بحث، لأنه مع كونه بظاهره مناقضا لما قدمه من بيان الخلاف إن أراد جواز اللعن الإجمالي بأن يقال: لعنة الله على قاتل الحسين أو الراضي به، فلا كلام فيه لقوله تعالى : أَلَا لَعْنَةُ اللهِ عَلَى الظَّلِمِينَ
It is not hidden what is in his transmission where he is vague about its speaker, then his reasoning requires proving his order to kill al-Husayn (may Allah be pleased with him) first, and then deducing his disbelief from it second, and both are prevented. For Hujjat al-Islam said in al-Ihya': 'If it is said: Is it permissible to curse Yazid because he killed al-Husayn or ordered it? We say: This has not been established at all, so it is not permissible to say that he killed him or ordered it, let alone cursing him, because it is not permissible to attribute a major sin to a Muslim without verification. Yes, it is permissible to say that Ibn Muljam killed 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) and Abu Lu'lu' killed 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him), for that is established by tawatur... So a Muslim should not be accused of fisq or kufr without verification.' And in summary, in cursing individuals there is danger, so it should be avoided, and there is no danger in refraining from cursing Iblis, let alone others. End quote. And because the order to kill al-Husayn (may Allah be pleased with him) does not necessitate disbelief, for killing non-prophets is a major sin according to Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah unless one considers it lawful, and it is not specific to al-Husayn and the like, and considering it lawful is something only the Majestic One knows. Rather, his killing is analogous to the killing of 'Ammar ibn Yasir. As for what some ignorant people have uttered that al-Husayn was a rebel (baghi), it is false according to Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, and perhaps this is from the deliriums of the Khawarij straying from the path. Then he said: And they agreed on the permissibility of cursing the one who killed him or ordered it or permitted it or was pleased with it, but there is discussion: because despite it apparently contradicting what he previously presented of the statement of disagreement, if he intends the permissibility of general cursing by saying: 'The curse of Allah be upon the killer of al-Husayn or the one pleased with it,' then there is no argument, because of His saying: 'Surely the curse of Allah is upon the wrongdoers.'
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a critique and further analysis by the author. He first criticizes the previous report (from Sharh al-'Aqa'id) for being vague about its source and for requiring unproven premises. He then cites Ghazali's Ihya' to support the view that one cannot attribute killing or ordering the killing of al-Husayn to Yazid without proof, and thus cannot curse him specifically. Ghazali distinguishes between cases established by tawatur (like the killers of 'Ali and 'Umar) and unproven accusations. The author then gives his own reasoning: ordering the killing of al-Husayn does not necessitate kufr because killing a non-prophet is a major sin, not kufr, unless one considers it lawful. He also refutes the claim that al-Husayn was a rebel. Finally, he addresses the statement that 'they agreed on permissibility of cursing the killer...' and explains that if it means general cursing (like 'curse of Allah upon the killer of al-Husayn'), it is permissible based on the Quranic verse. Thus, the passage clarifies the nuanced ruling on cursing specific individuals versus general condemnation.

Key Terms
لعن
يزيد
الحسين
تواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a historical report exists, it can be used to curse a person without verifying its chain or content.
Correction: Ghazali and the author insist that only facts established by tawatur or rigorous evidence can be used for accusations; otherwise it is forbidden.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali consider it permissible to mention that Ibn Muljam killed 'Ali but not that Yazid killed al-Husayn?
A) Because Ibn Muljam confessed
B) Because the killing of 'Ali is established by tawatur, while Yazid's involvement is not
C) Because 'Ali was a caliph and al-Husayn was not
D) Because the Quran mentions the killing of 'Ali

Key Takeaway
Accusing a Muslim of a major sin or disbelief requires certain proof; speculation is forbidden.

Cursing Yazid: General vs. Specific Condemnation
Author gives his own reasoning and ruling on cursing Yazid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that ordering the killing of al-Husayn is a major sin, not disbelief, and the ruling on cursing specific individuals versus general condemnation.

Core Idea
General curses against wrongdoers are allowed, but cursing specific individuals requires caution and does not entail disbelief.

The author continues his analysis by addressing a key question: does ordering the killing of al-Husayn constitute disbelief? He clarifies that killing a non-prophet is a major sin, not kufr, unless one considers it lawful (istihlal). He also refutes the false claim that al-Husayn was a rebel. Finally, he addresses a statement about consensus on cursing the killer, distinguishing between specific and general curses.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the distinction: major sin vs. disbelief
The author states that ordering the killing of al-Husayn does not necessitate kufr. Killing a non-prophet is a major sin, but it becomes kufr only if one believes it is lawful. This is a key theological nuance.
2
Refute the 'baghi' claim
The author dismisses the idea that al-Husayn was a rebel (baghi) as false and attributes it to the Khawarij. This defends the honor of the Prophet's grandson.
3
Understand the ruling on cursing
The author discusses the statement that 'they agreed on permissibility of cursing the killer...' He explains that if it means a general curse like 'curse of Allah upon the killer of al-Husayn', it is permissible based on Quran 7:44. But specific cursing of individuals remains problematic.
The author points out a contradiction if the statement meant specific cursing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يخفى ما في نقله حيث أبهم في قائله، ثم تعليله يحتاج إلى إثبات أمره بقتل الحسين رضي الله عنه أولاً ثم ترتب كفره عليه ثانياً، وكلاهما ممنوع. فقد قال حجة الإسلام في "الإحياء": «فإن قيل: هل يجوز لعن يزيد؛ لأنه قاتل الحسين أو آمر به؟ قلنا هذا لم يثبت أصلا، فلا يجوز أن يقال: إنه قتله أو أمر به فضلا عن اللعنة، لأنه لا تجوز نسبة مسلم إلى كبيرة من غير تحقيق. نعم يجوز أن يقال : قتل ابن ملجم عليا رضي الله عنه وقتل أبو لؤلؤة عمر رضي الله عنه، فإن ذلك ثبت متواترا... فلا يرمى مسلم بفسق أو كفر من غير تحقيق». وعلى الجملة ففي لعن الأشخاص خطر فليجتنب، ولا خطر في السكوت عن لعن إبليس فضلا عن غيره، انتهى. ولأن الأمر بقتل الحسين رضي الله عنه لا يوجب الكفر، فإن قتل غير الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام كبيرة عند أهل السنة والجماعة إلا أن يكون مستحلا، وهو غير مختص بالحسين ونحوه مع أن الاستحلال أمر لا يطلع عليه إلا ذو الجلال، وإنما كان قتله نظير قتل عمار بن ياسر. وأما ما تفوه به بعض الجهلة من أن الحسين كان باغيا فباطل عند أهل السنة والجماعة، ولعل هذا من هذيانات الخوارج عن الجادة. ثم قال: واتفقوا على جواز اللعن على من قتله أو أمر به أو أجازه أو رضي به، ففيه بحث، لأنه مع كونه بظاهره مناقضا لما قدمه من بيان الخلاف إن أراد جواز اللعن الإجمالي بأن يقال: لعنة الله على قاتل الحسين أو الراضي به، فلا كلام فيه لقوله تعالى : أَلَا لَعْنَةُ اللهِ عَلَى الظَّلِمِينَ
It is not hidden what is in his transmission where he is vague about its speaker, then his reasoning requires proving his order to kill al-Husayn (may Allah be pleased with him) first, and then deducing his disbelief from it second, and both are prevented. For Hujjat al-Islam said in al-Ihya': 'If it is said: Is it permissible to curse Yazid because he killed al-Husayn or ordered it? We say: This has not been established at all, so it is not permissible to say that he killed him or ordered it, let alone cursing him, because it is not permissible to attribute a major sin to a Muslim without verification. Yes, it is permissible to say that Ibn Muljam killed 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) and Abu Lu'lu' killed 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him), for that is established by tawatur... So a Muslim should not be accused of fisq or kufr without verification.' And in summary, in cursing individuals there is danger, so it should be avoided, and there is no danger in refraining from cursing Iblis, let alone others. End quote. And because the order to kill al-Husayn (may Allah be pleased with him) does not necessitate disbelief, for killing non-prophets is a major sin according to Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah unless one considers it lawful, and it is not specific to al-Husayn and the like, and considering it lawful is something only the Majestic One knows. Rather, his killing is analogous to the killing of 'Ammar ibn Yasir. As for what some ignorant people have uttered that al-Husayn was a rebel (baghi), it is false according to Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, and perhaps this is from the deliriums of the Khawarij straying from the path. Then he said: And they agreed on the permissibility of cursing the one who killed him or ordered it or permitted it or was pleased with it, but there is discussion: because despite it apparently contradicting what he previously presented of the statement of disagreement, if he intends the permissibility of general cursing by saying: 'The curse of Allah be upon the killer of al-Husayn or the one pleased with it,' then there is no argument, because of His saying: 'Surely the curse of Allah is upon the wrongdoers.'
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a critique and further analysis by the author. He first criticizes the previous report (from Sharh al-'Aqa'id) for being vague about its source and for requiring unproven premises. He then cites Ghazali's Ihya' to support the view that one cannot attribute killing or ordering the killing of al-Husayn to Yazid without proof, and thus cannot curse him specifically. Ghazali distinguishes between cases established by tawatur (like the killers of 'Ali and 'Umar) and unproven accusations. The author then gives his own reasoning: ordering the killing of al-Husayn does not necessitate kufr because killing a non-prophet is a major sin, not kufr, unless one considers it lawful. He also refutes the claim that al-Husayn was a rebel. Finally, he addresses the statement that 'they agreed on permissibility of cursing the killer...' and explains that if it means general cursing (like 'curse of Allah upon the killer of al-Husayn'), it is permissible based on the Quranic verse. Thus, the passage clarifies the nuanced ruling on cursing specific individuals versus general condemnation.

استحلال

Checkpoint
According to the author, when does killing a non-prophet become disbelief?
A) When the victim is a relative of the Prophet
B) When the killer is a ruler
C) When the killer considers it lawful (istihlal)
D) When the killing is unjust

Key Takeaway
Ordering the killing of al-Husayn is a major sin, not necessarily disbelief, and general curses against oppressors are permissible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about the oppression of al-Husayn, do you find yourself quick to verbally curse the individuals you think were responsible? How does this lesson's distinction between general and specific cursing challenge your reaction, and what will you do the next time that urge arises?
?
The author warns against repeating the false claim that al-Husayn was a rebel. Have you ever casually repeated a historical accusation against a figure without verifying its truth from reliable sources? What step will you take today to ensure you only speak about others—past or present—based on knowledge, not hearsay?
?
If you were certain that a past ruler committed a major sin but cannot be certain they considered it lawful (istihlal), does this lesson allow you to label them a disbeliever? How does this affect the way you view those you disagree with in your own life, and what will you change about how you judge others?

The Ruling on Cursing Yazeed Without Proof
Al-Ghazali's ruling on cursing Yazeed with conditions of proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ghazali prohibits cursing Yazeed based on lack of proof and the principle of not attributing sin without verification.

Core Idea
Cursing a Muslim without proof is a greater sin than the alleged sin itself.

This passage from Sharh al-Fiqh al-Akbar addresses a sensitive historical issue: whether it is permissible to curse Yazeed ibn Muawiya for his alleged role in the killing of al-Husayn. The author quotes Imam al-Ghazali's clear ruling that such an act cannot be proven, and therefore cursing is not allowed. Let us examine the text and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ghazali quote
Focus on the question and answer structure. Note that the claim about Yazeed is unproven.
Tawatur (mass transmission) is key to establishing certainty in historical reports.
2
Understand the prohibition
Al-Ghazali prohibits cursing because it involves accusing a Muslim of a major sin without certain knowledge.
3
Compare with proven cases
The cases of Ibn Muljam and Abu Lu'lu'a are proven by tawatur, so it is permissible to attribute the acts to them.
This shows the standard required.
4
Reflect on the danger of cursing
The author concludes that avoiding cursing individuals is safer, even for Iblis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد قال حجة الإسلام في "الإحياء": «فإن قيل: هل يجوز لعن يزيد؛ لأنه قاتل الحسين أو آمر به؟ قلنا هذا لم يثبت أصلا، فلا يجوز أن يقال: إنه قتله أو أمر به فضلا اللعنة، لأنه لا تجوز نسبة مسلم إلى كبيرة من غير تحقيق. نعم يجوز أن يقال : قتل ابن ملجم عليا رضي الله عنه وقتل أبو لؤلؤة عمر رضي الله عنه، فإن ذلك ثبت متواتر (١).. أن يرمى مسلم بفسق أو كفر من غير تحقيق». وعلى الجملة ففي لعن الأشخاص خطر فليجتنب، ولا خطر في السكوت عن لعن إبليس فضلا عن غيره، انتهى. ولأن الأمر بقتل الحسين رضي الله عنه لا يوجب الكفر، فإن قتل غير الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام كبيرة عند أهل السنة والجماعة إلا أن يكون مستحلا، وهو غير مختص بالحسين ونحوه مع أن الاستحلال أمر لا يطلع عليه إلا ذو الجلال، وإنما كان قتله نظير قتل عمار بن ياسر. وأما ما تفوه به بعض الجهلة من أن الحسين كان باغيا فباطل عند أهل السنة والجماعة، ولعل هذا من هذيانات الخوارج عن الجادة. (١) وقع هاهنا في نسخة شرح الفقه الأكبر الشائعة في بلادنا تحريف شديد فنقل فيها لفظ الإحياء هكذا: بل لا يجوز أن يقال إن ابن ملجم قتل عليا ولا أبو لؤلؤ قتل عمر فإن ذلك لم يثبت متواترا.هـ، وهو باطل كما لا يخفى، والصواب ما نقلتُ فليتنبه. (الزمزمة القمرية، ص: ٢٧)
The Proof of Islam said in al-Ihya: 'If it is said: Is it permissible to curse Yazeed because he killed al-Husayn or commanded it? We say: This has not been established at all, so it is not permissible to say that he killed him or commanded it, let alone curse him, because it is not permissible to attribute a major sin to a Muslim without verification. Yes, it is permissible to say: Ibn Muljam killed Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, and Abu Lu'lu'a killed Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, for that is established as mutawatir. ... that a Muslim be accused of wrongdoing or disbelief without verification.' In sum, in cursing individuals there is danger, so one should avoid it, and there is no danger in refraining from cursing Iblis, let alone others. End. And because commanding the killing of al-Husayn, may Allah be pleased with him, does not necessitate disbelief, for killing other than prophets, peace be upon them, is a major sin according to Ahl al-Sunna wal-Jama'a unless one considers it lawful, and that is not specific to al-Husayn and his likes, moreover considering it lawful is a matter that only the Majestic knows. Rather his killing was analogous to the killing of Ammar ibn Yasir. As for what some ignoramuses uttered that al-Husayn was a rebel, that is false according to Ahl al-Sunna wal-Jama'a, and perhaps this is from the delirious ramblings of the Khawarij who deviated from the path. (1) Here, in a copy of Sharh al-Fiqh al-Akbar widespread in our country, there is a severe corruption, in which the wording of al-Ihya was transmitted as: 'Rather, it is not permissible to say that Ibn Muljam killed Ali nor that Abu Lu'lu' killed Umar, for that is not established as mutawatir.' Etc. That is false, as is not hidden, and the correct is what I transmitted, so let one be attentive. (al-Zamzama al-Qamariyya, p. 27)
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents a theological argument regarding the ruling on cursing Yazeed ibn Muawiya. It begins by quoting al-Ghazali's Ihya, where he addresses the question of whether it is permissible to curse Yazeed for his alleged involvement in the killing of al-Husayn. Al-Ghazali states that the accusation has not been proven, so one cannot say that Yazeed killed or ordered the killing, let alone curse him. He analogizes that cursing a Muslim without verification is impermissible, but notes that it is permissible to say that Ibn Muljam killed Ali and Abu Lu'lu'a killed Umar because those are established by tawatur. The author then adds his own reasoning, explaining that even if the order to kill al-Husayn were proven, it would not constitute kufr, because killing a non-prophet is only a major sin (kabira) unless one considers it lawful (istihlal), which is an internal matter known only to Allah. The author then refutes the claim that al-Husayn was a baghi (rebel), stating it is false and attributes it to the Khawarij. Finally, the editor's note corrects a corrupted version of the Ihya quote that wrongly claimed the deaths of Ali and Umar were not mutawatir.

Key Terms
لعن
متواتر
كبيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is permissible to curse Yazeed because of his evil actions, even if not proven.
Correction: The passage teaches that cursing requires certain knowledge; without proof, it is prohibited to even attribute the act, let alone curse.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali, why is it permissible to say that Ibn Muljam killed Ali but not that Yazeed killed al-Husayn?
A) Because Ibn Muljam's act is proven by tawatur, while Yazeed's is not.
B) Because Ibn Muljam was a known enemy of Ali, while Yazeed was a caliph.
C) Because the killing of Ali was more historically significant.
D) Because Yazeed's involvement is well-known but not mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
One must not curse a Muslim without proof, as it involves accusing them of a major sin without certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you spoke negatively about someone—whether a historical figure or a living person—based on hearsay. How does al-Ghazali's insistence on 'certain knowledge' before cursing a Muslim expose the gravity of your words? Will you repent from that and commit to only speaking with certainty?
?
Is there a Muslim individual you have privately cursed in your heart, perhaps a public figure you dislike? According to this lesson, you are attributing a major sin to them without proof. What would it take for you to stop that habit right now?
?
You may not curse Yazeed aloud, but do you harbor contempt in your heart based on popular narratives? If the evidence is not mutawatir, your contempt is unjustified. Can you truly say you have no such hidden enmity? What will you do to cleanse your heart?

Refuting the Claim that al-Husayn was a Rebel
The author's expansion on the ruling of killing al-Husayn and refutation of the rebel claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the killing of al-Husayn is considered a major sin and not disbelief, and the refutation of the baghi claim.

Core Idea
The killing of al-Husayn is a major sin, but not kufr, and the claim that he was a rebel is a Kharijite delusion.

After establishing the prohibition of cursing without proof, the author turns to a related issue: the nature of the killing of al-Husayn. He argues that even if Yazeed ordered the killing, it would not be kufr but a major sin unless done with istihlal. He also strongly refutes the false claim that al-Husayn was a baghi (rebel), attributing it to the Khawarij.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's reasoning
The author argues that commanding the killing does not necessitate disbelief. Note the distinction between major sin and kufr.
The key concept is istihlal (deeming the act lawful).
2
Understand the condition of istihlal
Istihlal is an internal belief known only to Allah; without it, killing is only a major sin.
3
Consider the analogy with Ammar ibn Yasir
The killing of al-Husayn is compared to the killing of Ammar, which was also a major sin.
4
Examine the refutation of the baghi claim
The author declares it false and traces it to the Khawarij, who are known for deviant beliefs.

Source Text

The Proof of Islam said in al-Ihya: 'If it is said: Is it permissible to curse Yazeed because he killed al-Husayn or commanded it? We say: This has not been established at all, so it is not permissible to say that he killed him or commanded it, let alone curse him, because it is not permissible to attribute a major sin to a Muslim without verification. And because commanding the killing of al-Husayn, may Allah be pleased with him, does not necessitate disbelief, for killing other than prophets, peace be upon them, is a major sin according to Ahl al-Sunna wal-Jama'a unless one considers it lawful, and that is not specific to al-Husayn and his likes, moreover considering it lawful is a matter that only the Majestic knows. As for what some ignoramuses uttered that al-Husayn was a rebel, that is false according to Ahl al-Sunna wal-Jama'a, and perhaps this is from the delirious ramblings of the Khawarij who deviated from the path. Commentary
This passage presents a theological argument regarding the ruling on cursing Yazeed ibn Muawiya. It begins by quoting al-Ghazali's Ihya, where he addresses the question of whether it is permissible to curse Yazeed for his alleged involvement in the killing of al-Husayn. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Anyone involved in killing al-Husayn automatically becomes a disbeliever.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition would turn the killing of al-Husayn from a major sin into disbelief?
A) If the killer was a known enemy of the Prophet's family.
B) If the killer considered the act lawful (istihlal).
C) If the killing was ordered by a caliph.
D) If the killing was done during a battle.

Key Takeaway
The killing of al-Husayn is a heinous major sin, but it is not disbelief unless the perpetrator deems it lawful; the claim that al-Husayn was a rebel is a Kharijite falsehood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you quick to label someone's sin as disbelief, forgetting that only Allah knows the heart's intention (istihlal)? Examine your own tendency to declare takfir based on outward actions. What step can you take today to guard against this Kharijite mindset?
?
When you hear about a controversial event like Karbala, do you immediately side with one party and condemn the other, or do you adopt the balanced Ahl al-Sunna position that grieves the tragedy without making excuses for the perpetrators? What does your reaction reveal about your attachment to the Sunnah?
?
The Khawarij claimed al-Husayn was a rebel. Do you ever find yourself echoing similar extremist rhetoric, calling fellow Muslims rebels or traitors because they disagree with your political or religious views? How can you purge this Kharijite tendency from your heart starting now?

Distinguishing General and Specific Cursing
The author critiques a claim of agreement on cursing and distinguishes general from specific cursing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the crucial difference between general cursing (al-la'n al-ijmali) and specific cursing (al-la'n al-shakhsi), and why only the former is subject to agreement among scholars.

Core Idea
Not every curse is the same: general curses warn, specific curses accuse.

In this passage, Sanusi challenges a popular claim that scholars unanimously agree on cursing the killers of al-Husayn. He shows that such 'agreement' only applies to general, unspecific curses — not to specific individuals. This distinction is vital for understanding the limits of permissible speech in theological disputes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the initial claim
Start by reading the Arabic and translation. Identify the claim that the author is critiquing: that there is agreement on cursing the killer of al-Husayn.
2
Spot the author's critique
Notice how the author says 'fihi bahth' (it requires examination) because the apparent meaning contradicts earlier clarification of disagreement.
3
Understand the two types of cursing
The author distinguishes general cursing (la'n ijmali) from specific cursing (la'n shakhsi). Pay attention to how each is defined and what evidence supports them.
General cursing uses phrases like 'curse of Allah upon the wrongdoers', while specific cursing targets a named person.
4
Reflect on the 'secret' behind general curses
The author explains that general curses are not actually curses on individuals but prohibitions and warnings, as seen in Quran and hadith.
5
Connect to the conclusion
Why does this matter? The author concludes that claiming agreement on specific cursing is false — only general cursing is permissible by agreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال: واتفقوا على جواز اللعن على من قتله أو أمر به أو أجازه أو رضي به، ففيه بحث، لأنه مع كونه بظاهره مناقضا لما قدمه من بيان الخلاف إن أراد جواز اللعن الإجمالي بأن يقال: لعنة الله على قاتل الحسين أو الراضي به، فلا كلام فيه لقوله تعالى : أَلَا لَعْنَةُ اللهِ عَلَى الظَّلِمِينَ [هود: ١٨]، ولقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: ((لعن الله أكل الربا وموكله)). والسر فيه أن ذلك ليس لعنا على أحد في الحقيقة، بل هو نهي عن القتل الذي يترتب اللعن عليه وبيان لقبحه، وإيجابه بعد فاعله عن رحمة الله وشفاعة رسوله صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم، وإن أراد جواز اللعن الشخصي فقد تقدم عدم جوازه بلا اختلاف فيه فضلا عن اتفاقه.
Then he said: 'And they agreed on the permissibility of cursing whoever killed him, or commanded it, or permitted it, or was pleased with it.' In it there is discussion, because while its apparent meaning contradicts what he previously presented of the clarification of disagreement, if he intends the permissibility of general cursing by saying: 'Curse of Allah upon the killer of al-Husayn or the one pleased with it,' then there is no argument against it due to His saying, Exalted is He: 'Indeed the curse of Allah is upon the wrongdoers' [Hud 11:18], and due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Allah has cursed the consumer of usury and its payer.' The secret in that is that it is not actually a curse upon anyone in reality, but rather a prohibition of the killing that entails the curse and a statement of its ugliness, and a declaration that it places its doer beyond the mercy of Allah and the intercession of His Messenger, may Allah bless him and grant him peace. But if he intends the permissibility of specific cursing, then its impermissibility has already preceded without disagreement about it, let alone agreement.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of a statement claiming that there is agreement (ittifaq) on the permissibility of cursing anyone involved in the killing of al-Husayn. The author argues that this statement requires examination (fihi bahth). He notes that the apparent meaning contradicts earlier clarification of disagreement, indicating the statement is not universally accepted. Then he makes a key distinction: if 'cursing' here means general (ijmali) cursing—such as saying 'curse of Allah upon the killer of al-Husayn'—then it is permissible, supported by Quranic and hadith texts that curse categories of wrongdoers. The author explains that such general phrases are not actual curses on specific individuals but are warnings and expressions of divine displeasure. However, if the agreement is about specific (shakhsi) cursing of particular individuals, then the earlier discussion already established that it is impermissible without any disagreement, so claiming agreement is false.

Key Terms
لعن إجمالي
لعن شخصي
اتفاق
ظالمون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that cursing specific individuals involved in al-Husayn's death is universally accepted as permissible.
Correction: The author shows that only general curses (like against 'wrongdoers') are agreed upon; specific curses require proof of individual sin and are generally impermissible.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the claim of agreement on cursing the killer of al-Husayn?
A) He agrees it is valid and cites the Quran.
B) He rejects it because it contradicts earlier disagreement.
C) He says it is only valid if applied to specific individuals.
D) He says it applies only to those who gave orders.

Key Takeaway
General curses on categories like 'wrongdoers' are permissible and serve as warnings, but specific curses on named individuals are impermissible and not subject to agreement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are angry at a particular person who has wronged you, do you ever catch yourself desiring or uttering a curse upon them specifically? How does this lesson challenge you to refrain from that, even in your heart?
?
The passage teaches that general curses like 'curse upon wrongdoers' are really warnings against the act itself. Have you ever used religious language (e.g., 'Allah will punish them') to justify a personal grudge? How can you turn that into a sincere plea for guidance instead?
?
Consider the phrase 'it is not actually a curse upon anyone in reality but a prohibition.' How does that change your view of Allah's justice? Are you ready to see even your own sins as being warned against rather than as a cause for eternal despair? What will change in your heart today?

Refuting the Claim of Mutawatir Approval
Author refutes claim of mutawatir approval and clarifies it is fisq, not kufr.

Learning Objective
Understand why the author rejects the claim that Yazeed's approval of al-Husayn's killing is mutawatir in meaning and why such approval does not constitute kufr.

Core Idea
An action like approving of a killing is fisq, not kufr, unless combined with istihlal.

A controversial statement about Yazeed ibn Mu'awiya claims it is mutawatir that he approved of al-Husayn's killing, and that he should be cursed. The author dissects this claim, showing that it is neither established nor does it justify takfir. In this lesson, we examine the first part of his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overall sense of the refutation.
The passage contains a quoted statement followed by the author's critique.
2
Identify the quoted claim
Find the phrase 'الحق إن رضى يزيد...' and understand that it presents a claim of mutawatir meaning and a call for cursing.
This is what the author refutes.
3
Analyze the first objection
The author says 'لا يخفى أن قوله الحق... ليس في محله' because he says agreement was claimed but none exists.
The phrase 'الحق' is criticized as out of place.
4
Understand why approval is not kufr
Killing al-Husayn does not remove one from iman; it is fisq, not kufr, as long as istihlal is not present.
This is a key theological principle.
5
Reject the mutawatir claim
The author states that the approval has not been established at all, let alone through mutawatir transmission.
Thus the claim of tawatur is baseless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال بطريق المحاكمة في المقال : والحق إن رضى يزيد بقتل الحسين واستبشاره بذلك وإهانته أهل بيت النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم مما تواتر معناه، وإن كان تفاصيلها آحادا فنحن لا نتوقف في شأنه بل في إيمانه، لعنة الله عليه وعلى أنصاره وأعوانه. ولا يخفى أن قوله: "الحق" بعد نقله الاتفاق ليس في محله مع أن الرضى بقتل الحسين ليس بكفر لما سبق من أن قتله لا يوجب الخروج عن الإيمان بل هو فسق وخروج عن الطاعة إلى العصيان، ثم دعواه أنه مما تواتر معناه فقد سبق أنه لا يثبت أنه لا يثبت أصلا فضلا عن تواتر قطعا. ثم قوله : "لا نتوقف في شأنه بل في إيمانه" ، فقد علم مما تقدم أنه كان مسلما ولم يثبت عنه ما يخرجه عن كونه مؤمنا مع أن الاستحلال الموجب للكفر أمر باطني لا يعلمه إلا الله، فعدم توقفه ووجود جرأته خارج عن مقتضى عقله وعدالته وكمال علمه وجمال ديانته على أن العبرة بالخواتيم.
Then he said by way of arbitration in the article: 'The truth is that Yazeed's approval of the killing of al-Husayn, his joy at that, and his insulting the family of the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, are things whose meaning is mutawatir, even if its details are ahad. So we do not hesitate regarding his affair, but regarding his faith. The curse of Allah be upon him and his supporters and helpers.' It is not hidden that his saying 'the truth' after his transmission of agreement is out of place, while approval of the killing of al-Husayn is not kufr according to what preceded: that his killing does not necessitate exiting faith, but rather it is fisq and exiting from obedience to disobedience. Then his claim that its meaning is mutawatir—it has already preceded that it is not established at all, let alone being mutawatir definitively. Then his saying 'we do not hesitate regarding his affair, but regarding his faith'—it has been known from what preceded that he was a Muslim and nothing has been established from him that would remove him from being a believer, while istihlal that necessitates kufr is an internal matter known only by Allah. So his lack of hesitation and his audacity are outside the requirements of his intellect, justice, completeness of knowledge, and beauty of religion, especially since the criterion is by the endings.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a statement that declares Yazeed's approval of al-Husayn's killing to be mutawatir in meaning and that he should be cursed, with the speaker expressing certainty about Yazeed's evil but uncertainty about his faith ('we don't hesitate regarding his affair, but regarding his faith'). The author criticizes this point by point. First, saying 'the truth' after claiming agreement is misplaced because the author has already shown no agreement exists. Second, even if approval is proven, it is not kufr but fisq (sin), as killing a non-prophet does not remove one from iman. Third, the claim that the approval is mutawatir is unfounded; the author asserts it is not established at all. Fourth, the suggestion of hesitation about Yazeed's faith is wrong because Yazeed was outwardly Muslim and nothing proven removes him from iman; internal istihlal (considering sin lawful) which would cause kufr is unknowable except to Allah. The author concludes that such a stance reflects poor judgment, and one should remember that the final state (khawatim) matters, not just past actions.

Key Terms
رضي
متواتر المعنى
آحاد
فسق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Being pleased with the killing of al-Husayn automatically makes one a kafir.
Correction: The author explains that such approval is fisq (sin), not kufr, as killing a non-prophet does not remove iman unless combined with istihlal (considering it lawful).

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that the claim of Yazeed's approval being mutawatir is not acceptable?
A) Because it is a known historical fact.
B) Because only the details are mutawatir.
C) Because it has not been established at all.
D) Because it is supported by a hadith.

Key Takeaway
The claim that Yazeed's approval is mutawatir is baseless, and even if true, it is sin not disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been absolutely certain that someone’s actions proved their disbelief, only to find out later that you judged without evidence? How does this lesson’s insistence on verifying transmission (tawatur) and distinguishing fisq from kufr force you to rethink that certainty? What will you do next time you are tempted to pass judgment on another’s faith?
?
The author calls out the phrase 'the truth' when there is no real agreement. Are you holding onto beliefs or opinions as 'the truth' simply because you heard them repeated, without verifying their basis? Which belief in your life might be built on a false claim of consensus? Name it and commit to re-examining it today.
?
This lesson says that even grave sin does not automatically expel a person from faith. Is there someone you have mentally written off as beyond Allah’s mercy because of their actions? How does the author’s distinction between fisq and kufr challenge your willingness to see that person as still having iman? Will you change how you treat them starting now?

Refuting Hesitation on Yazeed's Faith
Author refutes hesitation about Yazeed's faith, emphasizing lack of proof and internal nature of istihlal.

Learning Objective
Understand why the author rejects any hesitation about Yazeed's faith and emphasizes that takfir requires established proof and internal istihlal is unknown.

Core Idea
Takfir requires clear proof; internal intentions are known only to Allah; the final state matters.

The controversial statement concludes that 'we do not hesitate regarding his affair, but regarding his faith.' The author strongly refutes this, asserting that Yazeed was outwardly Muslim and no evidence removes him from iman. This lesson explores the principles that guard against unjust takfir.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the response to 'لا نتوقف في شأنه بل في إيمانه'.
This part deals with the hesitation about faith.
2
Understand the rebuttal of hesitation
The author says Yazeed was Muslim and nothing has been proven to remove him from faith.
This establishes the default of iman.
3
Learn about istihlal
Istihlal (considering sin lawful) is what causes kufr, but it is internal and known only to Allah.
Thus we cannot judge that Yazeed committed istihlal.
4
Recognize the audacity of the claim
The author criticizes the lack of hesitation and audacity as contrary to reason, justice, and piety.
This shows the gravity of baseless takfir.
5
Apply the principle of 'al-ibrah bi al-khawatim'
The criterion is the final state; we should not base judgments on past actions alone.
This is a crucial principle in judging anyone's faith.

Disagreement on Takfir of Yazeed
Ibn al-Humam reports two opinions on takfir Yazeed with evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the basis for the scholarly disagreement on whether Yazeed is considered a disbeliever, including the evidence cited by those who held that view.

Core Idea
Takfir of Yazeed is not a settled matter; it depends on disputed evidence.

Ibn al-Humam reports a scholarly disagreement about Yazeed's status as a believer or disbeliever. The passage presents the evidence that some scholars used to declare him a disbeliever. Understanding this disagreement helps clarify that takfir is a serious matter requiring clear proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the tone of reporting disagreement.
The passage is short but packed with a claim and two pieces of evidence.
2
Identify the two pieces of evidence
The first is that Yazeed declared wine lawful. The second is his statement after Karbala about avenging the polytheists of Badr.
Both actions can indicate istihlal of what is forbidden, which is kufr.
3
Consider why this leads to takfir
In Islamic theology, deeming something halal that is clearly haram (like wine or murder) can be an act of disbelief.
However, the interpretation of Yazeed's words is contested.
4
Note the reference to Imam Ahmad
Ibn al-Humam suggests this evidence may be the interpretation of a statement by Imam Ahmad.
This shows the complexity of attributing takfir to historical figures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن الهمام رحمه الله : واختلف في إكفار يزيد، قيل: نعم، يعني لما ورد عنه ما يدل على إكفاره من تحليل الخمر ومن تفوّهه بعد قتل الحسين وأصحابه: إني جازيتهم بما فعلوا بأشياخ قريش وصناديدهم في بدر وأمثال ذلك، ولعله وجه ما قال الإمام أحمد رحمه الله (١) "المسند" للإمام أحمد بن حنبل، مسند عبد الله بن مسعود ٤٥/٢، حديث: ٣٧٢٥.
Ibn al-Humam, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'There is disagreement regarding declaring Yazeed a disbeliever. It was said: Yes, meaning because there came from him what indicates his disbelief, such as declaring wine lawful and his utterance after the killing of al-Husayn and his companions: I have recompensed them for what they did to the elders of Quraysh and their champions at Badr and the like. And perhaps that is the interpretation of what Imam Ahmad, may Allah have mercy on him, said. (1) al-Musnad by Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal, Musnad of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud 2/45, hadith 3725.'
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a report of a scholarly disagreement (ikhtilaf) on the matter of takfir of Yazeed. Ibn al-Humam states that some scholars said Yazeed is a disbeliever based on reports that he declared wine lawful (tahlil al-khamr) and made a statement after the battle of Karbala that he had taken revenge for the polytheists of Quraysh killed at Badr, implying that he considered the killing of al-Husayn as vengeance. Such statements indicate istihlal of murder, which is kufr. Ibn al-Humam notes that this might be the interpretation of a statement by Imam Ahmad. The passage also includes a footnote citing the Musnad for a hadith, likely providing evidence for the general principle of cursing or similar. This passage presents the evidence for those who held the opinion of takfir, but the author of the commentary earlier argued against it.

Key Terms
إكفار
تحليل الخمر
جازيتهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that takfir of Yazeed is a settled matter agreed upon by all scholars.
Correction: Ibn al-Humam explicitly reports that there is disagreement (ikhtilaf), and the passage only gives evidence for those who said 'yes'.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn al-Humam, what was one reason some scholars declared Yazeed a disbeliever?
A) He fought against the Prophet's family.
B) He declared wine lawful and spoke of avenging polytheists.
C) He did not perform the five daily prayers.
D) He refused to accept the caliphate.

Key Takeaway
Scholarly disagreement exists on Yazeed's disbelief, with some citing his declaration of wine lawful and revenge statement as evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about historical figures like Yazeed, do you find yourself quick to judge their faith based on incomplete reports? How will you train yourself to withhold judgment when even scholars disagreed?
?
The evidence of 'declaring wine lawful' is cited as proof of disbelief. Are there areas in your own life where you have made what Allah forbade seem permissible to yourself? What step will you take today to sincerely repent from that?
?
Before this lesson, did you assume that takfir of Yazeed was a settled issue? Which of your assumptions about other Muslims might need to be re-examined in light of scholarly disagreement?

Yazeed's Satisfaction and the Limits of Takfir
The commentator refutes the claim that Yazeed's satisfaction with Hussain's killing is kufr and argues his iman is intact.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's refutation of the argument that Yazeed's satisfaction with Hussain's killing constitutes disbelief, and the distinction between sin and disbelief.

Core Idea
Satisfaction with a sin does not necessarily remove one from faith.

This passage presents the commentator's careful refutation of a claim that Yazeed's satisfaction with Hussain's killing makes him a disbeliever. The commentator distinguishes between sin and disbelief, critiques the claim of tawatur, and emphasizes that internal acts like considering something lawful are known only to God. We will explore these key arguments and their implications for judging faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the commentator's own rhythm come through before interpreting.
2
Identify the claim being refuted
Find the statement that the commentator criticizes. Look for the phrase 'the truth is that...' and see how the commentator challenges it.
3
Analyze the refutation's structure
The commentator makes several points: (1) satisfaction is not kufr, (2) tawatur is not established, (3) Yazeed was a Muslim with no proven act of kufr, (4) istihlal is internal and unknown. Notice the logical flow.
This step corresponds to the guided breakdown.
4
Reflect on the principle of final ends
Consider the commentator's closing point: 'the consideration is of final ends' (al-ʻibra bi al-khawātim). Why is this important in judging a person's faith?
This connects to the broader theological principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال بطريق المحاكمة في المقال : والحق إن رضى يزيد بقتل الحسين واستبشاره بذلك وإهانته أهل بيت النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم مما تواتر معناه، وإن كان تفاصيلها آحادا فنحن لا نتوقف في شأنه بل في إيمانه، لعنة الله عليه وعلى أنصاره وأعوانه. ولا يخفى أن قوله: "و"الحق" بعد نقله الاتفاق ليس في محله مع أن الرضى بقتل الحسين ليس بكفر لما سبق من أن قتله لا يوجب الخروج عن الإيمان بل هو فسق وخروج عن إلى العصيان، ثم دعواه أنه مما تواتر معناه فقد سبق أنه لا يثبت أنه لا يثبت أصلا فضلا عن تواتر قطعا. ثم قوله : "لا نتوقف في شأنه بل في إيمانه" ، فقد علم مما تقدم أنه كان مسلما ولم يثبت عنه ما يخرجه عن كونه مؤمنا مع أن الاستحلال الموجب للكفر أمر باطني لا يعلمه إلا الله، فعدم توقفه ووجود جرأته خارج عن مقتضى عقله وعدالته وكمال علمه وجمال ديانته على أن العبرة بالخواتيم.
Then he said by way of arbitration in the article: The truth is that Yazeed's satisfaction with the killing of al-Husayn, his rejoicing at that, and his insulting the family of the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, is something whose meaning is mutawatir, even if its details are ahad; so we do not hesitate about his affair [i.e., his guilt] but about his faith. May the curse of God be upon him and upon his supporters and helpers. It is not hidden that his saying 'the truth' after reporting the consensus is not in its place, since satisfaction with killing al-Husayn is not disbelief, due to what preceded: that his killing does not necessitate exiting from faith, but rather is sin and exiting into disobedience. Then his claim that it is mutawatir in meaning – it has already preceded that it is not established at all, let alone being mutawatir definitively. Then his saying, 'We do not hesitate about his affair but about his faith' – it has been known from what preceded that he was a Muslim and nothing has been established from him that would take him out of being a believer, especially since considering something lawful that necessitates disbelief is an internal matter known only to God. So his lack of hesitation and the existence of his audacity is outside the requirement of his intellect, his uprightness, his complete knowledge, and the beauty of his piety, while the consideration is of final ends.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by the commentator of an argument made by someone who claimed that Yazeed's satisfaction with the killing of al-Husayn, his rejoicing, and his insulting the Prophet's family are mutawatir in meaning, and therefore Yazeed should be cursed. The commentator first rejects the claim to mutawatir, saying it is not proven. Then he argues that even if Yazeed was satisfied with the killing, that does not constitute disbelief (kufr) because killing itself does not remove one from faith; it is instead sin (fisq) and disobedience. He further explains that the person making the claim says, 'We do not hesitate about his affair but about his faith,' but the commentator retorts that it is established that Yazeed was a Muslim and nothing has been proven to take him out of faith, because the act of considering something lawful that causes disbelief is an internal matter known only to God. Therefore, the audacity to curse him contradicts the requirements of reason and justice. The commentator concludes that the final consideration is of one's end (al-ʻibra bi al-khawātim).

Key Terms

رضى
قتل
إيمان
تكفير
فسق
استحلال
متواتر
خاتمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that satisfaction with a sinful act (like Yazeed's satisfaction with Hussain's killing) necessarily constitutes disbelief (kufr) that removes one from the fold of Islam.
Correction: Satisfaction with a sin does not automatically make one a disbeliever; it may be a sin (fisq) but not necessarily kufr. Disbelief requires internal conviction or explicit rejection of faith, which is not established from Yazeed.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why does Yazeed's satisfaction with the killing of al-Husayn not constitute disbelief (kufr)?
A) Because satisfaction does not necessitate exiting from faith; it is only sin.
B) Because Yazeed was a companion and companions cannot commit disbelief.
C) Because the killing was justified.
D) Because Yazeed repented.

Key Takeaway
The commentator demonstrates that sin does not automatically imply disbelief, and that internal matters of intention are known only to Allah, so we cannot make definitive judgments about another's faith based solely on sinful actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel satisfaction at the misfortune of someone you consider sinful, does that satisfaction reveal something about your own iman? This lesson shows that satisfaction alone is not kufr—but what does it say about the state of your heart? What will you do to purify your inner reactions?
?
Have you ever judged someone’s faith based on an outward sin, assuming they must be a disbeliever? How does the lesson’s insistence that internal istihlal is known only to Allah challenge you to repent from that judgment? What concrete step can you take to leave final judgment to Allah alone?
?
The lesson ends with 'al-ibra bi al-khawatim' — consideration is of final ends. Imagine you died in a state of major sin: would you want others to assume you are a kafir? This principle gives hope but also accountability. Right now, what is one habit you need to change so that your final end is with iman?

The Scholarly Disagreement on Takfir of Yazeed
Ibn al-Humam reports the disagreement among scholars on whether to declare Yazeed a disbeliever.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two scholarly opinions on the takfir of Yazeed and the principle of suspending judgment (tawaqquf).

Core Idea
When evidence is unclear, suspend judgment and leave the matter to Allah.

Ibn al-Humam presents a scholarly disagreement (ikhtilaf) regarding the takfir of Yazeed. He reports two positions: one that declares him a disbeliever based on certain reports, and another that withholds judgment due to lack of conclusive proof. He then concludes with the correct stance of tawaqquf—suspending judgment and referring the matter to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Let the author's report of the disagreement come through.
2
Identify the two opinions
Find where Ibn al-Humam presents the first opinion ('qila: na'am') and the second ('qila: la'). Note the evidence cited for each.
3
Understand the basis of Imam Ahmad's position
Ibn al-Humam clarifies that Imam Ahmad's takfir was based on established reports of Yazeed's decisions, not merely his actions toward the Prophet's family.
4
Grasp the principle of tawaqquf
Notice the conclusion: 'the reality of the matter is to suspend judgment and refer his affair to God.' This is the recommended stance when evidence is contested.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this disagreement teaches the importance of caution and evidence in matters of faith and disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن الهمام رحمه الله : واختلف في إكفار يزيد، قيل: نعم، يعني لما ورد عنه ما يدل على إكفاره من تحليل الخمر ومن تفوّهه بعد قتل الحسين وأصحابه: إني جازيتهم بما فعلوا بأشياخ قريش وصناديدهم في بدر وأمثال ذلك، ولعله وجه ما قال الإمام أحمد رحمه الله بتكفيره بما ثبت عنده من نقل تقريره لا لما وقع عنه من الاجتراء على الذرية الطاهرة كالأمر بقتل الحسين وما جرى مما ينبو عن سماعه الطبع ويصمّ لما ذكره السمع كما علل به شارح كلامه، فإنه ليس على وفق مرامه كما قدمناه في لعنه، وقيل: لا، إذ لم يثبت لنا عنه تلك الأسباب الموجبة، أي لكفره وحقيقة الأمر التوقف فيه ومرجع أمره إلى الله سبحانه.
Ibn al-Humam, may God have mercy on him, said: There is disagreement on declaring Yazeed a disbeliever. It is said: Yes, meaning because what indicates his disbelief has been reported from him, such as his declaring wine lawful and his utterance after the killing of al-Husayn and his companions: 'I have rewarded them for what they did to the elders of Quraysh and their champions at Badr and the like.' Perhaps that is the basis of what Imam Ahmad, may God have mercy on him, said in declaring him a disbeliever, based on what was established with him of the transmission of his decision, not because of what occurred from him of audacity toward the pure progeny, such as the order to kill al-Husayn and what happened that the nature recoils from hearing and deafens the ear, as the commentator of his words reasoned; for it is not in accordance with his intent, as we have already mentioned regarding cursing him. And it is said: No, because those causes necessitating his disbelief are not established to us; and the reality of the matter is to suspend judgment, and his affair is referred to God, exalted be He.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a report from Ibn al-Humam about the scholarly disagreement (ikhtilaf) on declaring Yazeed a disbeliever (takfir). He presents two opinions. The first opinion, that Yazeed is a disbeliever, is based on reports that he declared wine lawful and said after the killing of al-Husayn that he rewarded them for the Battle of Badr. Ibn al-Humam suggests this is what Imam Ahmad relied on, not the audacity toward the family of the Prophet. The second opinion, that he is not a disbeliever, holds that the evidence for those causes is not established. Ibn al-Humam concludes that the correct position is to suspend judgment (tawaqquf) and leave the matter to God.

Key Terms
إكفار
تكفير
تحليل الخمر
تقرير
اجتراء
توقف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that declaring Yazeed a disbeliever is obligatory based on his actions against the Prophet's family.
Correction: Ibn al-Humam clarifies that Imam Ahmad's takfir was based on specific established reports, not merely on audacity, and that the correct position is to suspend judgment due to lack of confirmed evidence.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn al-Humam, what is the correct position regarding Yazeed's faith?
A) He is definitely a disbeliever.
B) He is definitely a believer.
C) Suspending judgment and referring his affair to God.
D) He is a hypocrite.

Key Takeaway
When evidence for takfir is disputed, the safe and just position is to suspend judgment and leave the matter to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged someone based on unverified reports, only to later learn the full story? How does tawaqquf (suspending judgment) challenge your natural inclination to form quick opinions about others, especially those you disagree with? Are you willing to leave their affair to Allah and refuse to pronounce takfir without clear evidence?
?
The scholars disagreed on Yazeed, but the safe position was to stop and remain silent on matters beyond certain knowledge. When you hear a controversial claim about a historical figure or a contemporary Muslim, do you pause to verify evidence, or do you immediately adopt the view of a particular scholar? What steps will you take this week to implement tawaqquf in a real-life situation?
?
Ibn al-Humam noted that even Imam Ahmad's takfir was based on established transmission, not on emotional reaction. Reflect on your own emotional reactions when you read about historical wrongdoings. Do your judgments stem from solid evidence or from outrage? Will you repent for times you allowed your anger to dictate your theological stance?

Ruling on Cursing Yazid and the Major Sinner
The author discusses the ruling on cursing Yazid and the principle of not cursing the major sinner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic ruling on cursing specific individuals like Yazid and the broader principle that a person who commits a major sin should not be cursed because his faith remains intact.

Core Idea
Faith persists with major sins; cursing is reserved for disbelief proven by definitive evidence.

This passage addresses two interconnected issues: the specific case of cursing Yazid ibn Mu'awiya and the general rule regarding those who commit major sins. The author draws on the Hanafi tradition to emphasize that faith is not negated by sin alone, and that takfir (declaring someone an unbeliever) requires certainty. As you read, notice how the author builds his argument from principles about faith and sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
2
Identify the two main discussions
Look for where the author talks about Yazid specifically and where he quotes al-Qunawi on the committer of a major sin.
These are two distinct rulings: one about a specific historical figure, one about a general category.
3
Examine the author’s use of sources
Notice how the author uses al-Qunawi’s quote and then checks the authenticity of a narration from Abu Hanifa. Consider why he includes this verification.
The author is careful about evidence.
4
Reflect on the distinction between disbelief and sin
The author ends by discussing the belief of the Qadariyya. Ask yourself: why does he clarify that it is a major sin, not disbelief?
This reinforces the principle of not making takfir for sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بتكفيره بما ثبت عنده من نقل تقريره لا لما وقع عنه من الاجتراء على الذرية الطاهرة كالأمر بقتل الحسين وما جرى مما ينبو عن سماعه الطبع ويصمّ لما ذكره السمع كما علل به شارح كلامه، فإنه ليس على وفق مرامه كما قدمناه في لعنه، وقيل: لا، إذ لم يثبت لنا عنه تلك الأسباب الموجبة، أي لكفره وحقيقة الأمر التوقف فيه ومرجع أمره إلى الله سبحانه. وقال القونوي في "شرح عمدة النسفي": ولا يلعن صاحب الكبيرة؛ لأن إيمانه معه ولم ينقص بارتكابه الكبيرة والمؤمن لا يجوز لعنه انتهى. ولا يخفى أن إيمان يزيد محقق ولا يثبت كفره بدليل ظني فضلا عن دليل قطعي، لعنه فلا يجوز بخصوصه . وأما ما نقله القونوي حيث قال: قد ذكر أبو حنيفة رحمه الله في "الفقه الأكبر" أن أبا حنيفة رحمه الله سئل عن الخوارج المحكمة؟ فقال: هم أخبث الخوارج، فقيل: أنكفّرهم؟ فقال : لا . ولكن نقاتلهم على ما قاتلهم الأئمة من أهل الخير كعلي بن أبي طالب وعمر بن عبد العزيز» فما وجدناه في النسخ المصححة ولا في الأصول المعتبرة. ثم قال القونوي وفي قوله بذنب إشارة إلى تكفيره بفساد اعتقاده كفساد اعتقاد المجسمة والمشبهة والقدرية ونحوهم؛ لأن ذلك لا يسمى ذنبا والكلام في الذنب، انتهى. ولا يخفى أن اعتقاد القدرية لا يعد من الأمور الكفرية، بل يعد من كبائر الذنوب وأقبحها حيث لا توبة للمبتدع.
By deeming him an unbeliever based on what was established with him from the transmission of his decision, not due to what occurred from him of audacity against the pure progeny, like the command to kill al-Husayn and what happened that nature shrinks from hearing and ears become deaf to, as the commentator of his speech explained, for it is not in accordance with his intent as we presented earlier regarding cursing him. And it was said: No, because those causes that necessitate his disbelief have not been established to us from him, and the reality of the matter is to refrain, and his affair is referred to Allah the Exalted. And al-Qunawi said in Sharh 'Umdat al-Nasafi: "One should not curse the committer of a major sin, because his faith is with him and does not decrease by committing the major sin, and the believer cannot be cursed." End quote. And it is not hidden that the faith of Yazid is verified and his disbelief is not established by a speculative proof, let alone a definitive proof, so cursing him specifically is not permissible. As for what al-Qunawi narrated, where he said: Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, stated in al-Fiqh al-Akbar that Abu Hanifa was asked about the Khawarij al-Muhakkima? He said: They are the worst of the Khawarij. It was said: Do we declare them unbelievers? He said: No. But we fight them as the imams of good, like 'Ali ibn Abi Talib and 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz, fought them. But we did not find it in the corrected copies nor in the reliable sources. Then al-Qunawi said: And in his statement "by a sin" is an indication of declaring him an unbeliever due to corruption of his belief, like the corruption of belief of the Mujassima, Mushabbiha, Qadariyya and the like; because that is not called a sin, and the discussion is about sin. End quote. And it is not hidden that the belief of the Qadariyya is not considered among the matters of disbelief, but rather is considered among the major sins and the ugliest of them, since there is no repentance for the innovator.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the permissibility of cursing Yazid and the general principle of not cursing the major sinner. The author first addresses the justification for declaring Yazid an unbeliever. He states that any ruling of disbelief should be based on established reports of his actions, not on hearsay about his audacity against the Prophet's family, such as the command to kill al-Husayn. The author notes that even the commentator attempted to justify cursing him but that reasoning is incorrect. Some scholars said we should refrain because the requisite causes for disbelief have not been confirmed. The true ruling is to suspend judgment and leave his affair to Allah. The author then quotes al-Qunawi, who cites the principle that the committer of a major sin should not be cursed because his faith remains intact; a believer cannot be cursed. Applying this, the author affirms that Yazid's faith is confirmed, and his disbelief cannot be established by speculative proof, so specific cursing is impermissible. The author then addresses a reported narration from Abu Hanifa about the Khawarij, but notes it is not found in reliable copies. Finally, al-Qunawi's discussion is mentioned: if someone is declared an unbeliever due to corrupt belief (like the Mujassima, Mushabbiha, Qadariyya), that corrupt belief is not called a 'sin'—the discussion is about sin. The author clarifies that the Qadariyya's belief is not disbelief but a major sin, and there is no repentance for the innovator. Thus, the passage distinguishes between disbelief and sin, and emphasizes caution in takfir and cursing.

Key Terms
لعن
تكفير
إيمان
صاحب الكبيرة
القدرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that committing a major sin (like a heinous act) automatically makes a person an unbeliever who can be cursed.
Correction: The passage clarifies that faith remains unless disbelief is proven by definitive evidence; thus, a major sinner's faith is intact and cursing is not permissible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on cursing the committer of a major sin (sahib al-kabira)?
A) It is permissible because his sin makes him deserving of curse.
B) It is not permissible because his faith remains with him.
C) It is obligatory to curse him to deter others.
D) It is permissible only if the sin is public and notorious.

Key Takeaway
Faith remains with the major sinner; therefore, they should not be cursed, and disbelief must be proven by certain evidence, not assumed from sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely encountered a person who committed a heinous act, maybe even someone you know. Did you ever think that their faith must be gone? This lesson teaches that faith remains with the major sinner, and they should not be cursed. How will you remember this when you hear of such acts, and what will you do to check your own heart from jumping to curse or declare disbelief?
?
Speculate: there might be a group of people you consider to be misguided in their beliefs, like those with Qadari-like tendencies. Did you ever categorize them as disbelievers without realizing? This lesson says their belief is a major sin, not disbelief. How will you change your attitude towards them, and what will you do to seek clarity before making such a grave judgment?
?
Think about your own sins. Do you ever feel that because you continue to sin, your iman must be diminished or lost? According to this lesson, that is not the case. How does the certainty that iman remains with you despite your shortcomings change the way you view yourself and your relationship with Allah? What step will you take to act on that hope instead of despair?

The Birth of the Mu'tazila
The author narrates the origin of the Mu'tazila from Wasil ibn 'Ata's departure from Hasan al-Basri.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical origin of the Mu'tazila sect and their defining doctrinal positions as presented in this passage.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila began with a split over the status of the grave sinner.

This lesson traces the birth of the Mu'tazila from a single classroom disagreement. You will see how a debate about sin led to a whole new theological school with distinctive doctrines on justice and divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dramatic moment when Hasan al-Basri says 'He has withdrawn from us.'
2
Identify the key doctrine that caused the split
The passage mentions 'al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn' (the intermediate state). Understand what this means: the grave sinner is neither believer nor disbeliever.
This doctrine directly contradicts the Sunni view that sin does not nullify faith.
3
Understand the two doctrines they championed
The Mu'tazila called themselves 'people of justice and unity' because they held that reward/punishment are obligatory on Allah (justice) and they denied eternal attributes (unity). Compare these with Sunni positions.
These are the core theological innovations that defined the Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأصل هذه المنازعة أن رئيس المعتزلة واصل بن عطاء اعتزل مجلس الحسن البصري رضي الله عنه يقرر أن مرتكب الكبيرة ليس بمؤمن ولا كافر وأثبت المنزلة بين المنزلتين، فقال الحسن رضي الله عنه : «قد اعتزل عنا». فسُمّوا المعتزلة وهم سموا أنفسهم أصحاب العدل والتوحيد لقولهم بوجوب ثواب المطيع وعقاب العاصي على الله سبحانه ونفي الصفات القديمة عنه، ثم إنهم توغلوا في علم الكلام وتشبثوا بأذيال الفلاسفة في كثير من الأصول، وشاع مذهبهم فيما بين الناس
And the origin of this dispute is that the head of the Mu'tazila, Wasil ibn 'Ata, withdrew from the circle of Hasan al-Basri, may Allah be pleased with him, asserting that the committer of a major sin is neither a believer nor a disbeliever, and established the intermediate state (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn). So Hasan al-Basri said: "He has withdrawn from us." Thus they were called Mu'tazila, and they called themselves the people of justice (adl) and unity (tawhid) because of their saying that reward for the obedient and punishment for the disobedient are obligatory upon Allah the Exalted, and their denial of the eternal attributes (sifat al-qadima) from Him. Then they delved into ilm al-kalam and clung to the tails of the philosophers in many principles, and their sect became widespread among the people.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative explaining the origin of the Mu'tazila sect. The author traces the dispute about the grave sinner to Wasil ibn 'Ata, who left (i'tazala) the teaching circle of Hasan al-Basri because he proposed that the grave sinner is neither a believer nor a disbeliever, but rather in an intermediate state (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn). Hasan al-Basri famously said, 'He has withdrawn from us,' giving the group their name 'Mu'tazila' (the withdrawers). The author notes that the Mu'tazila called themselves 'the people of justice and unity' (ashab al-adl wa al-tawhid) because they held that reward and punishment are rationally obligatory upon Allah (a denial of His absolute will), and they denied the eternal attributes of Allah (such as knowledge, power, etc.), which they considered distinct from His essence. The passage then states that they delved deeply into kalam and adopted many philosophical principles, leading to the spread of their sect among people. This sets the stage for the later conflict with Ahl al-Sunna.

Key Terms
معتزلة
واصل بن عطاء
الحسن البصري
المنزلة بين المنزلتين
أصحاب العدل والتوحيد
نفي الصفات القديمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Mu'tazila were moderates on the grave sinner issue, but they also held radical views on divine attributes and human responsibility.
Correction: The passage shows that besides their intermediate position on sin, they also denied eternal attributes and held that reward/punishment are obligatory on Allah, which are far from moderate in Sunni theology.

Checkpoint
Why were the followers of Wasil ibn 'Ata called Mu'tazila?
A) Because they withdrew from the circle of Hasan al-Basri.
B) Because they denied the eternal attributes of Allah.
C) Because they believed in free will.
D) Because they advocated for justice.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila sect originated from a single disagreement about the grave sinner, which led to a distinct school with doctrines on divine justice and negation of eternal attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila prided themselves on their rational approach, but it led them away from the consensus of the early community. Is there an area in your life where you are tempted to rely on your own reasoning over clear revelation or scholarly consensus? What steps can you take to submit to the truth even when it doesn't make sense to you?
?
Wasil ibn 'Ata's disagreement with his teacher over one issue led to the formation of a whole sect. Are there beliefs or practices where you have separated yourself from the mainstream of Ahl al-Sunna? What would it take to humble yourself and return, even if you still have questions?
?
The Mu'tazila called themselves 'people of justice and unity,' yet their doctrines contradicted the understanding of the Salaf. Could your own desire to appear 'enlightened' or 'reasonable' be causing you to adopt positions that are actually further from the truth? Examine your heart: are you seeking the truth or seeking to be different?
